<p>The City Municipality of <b>Bremen</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Stadtgemeinde Bremen</span>, <small>IPA:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈbʁeːmən]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Bremen.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Bremen.ogg" class="internal" title="Bremen.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>) is a <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic</a> city in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, which belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_(state)" title="Bremen (state)">Free Hanseatic City of Bremen</a> (also called just "Bremen" for short), a federal state of Germany.</p>
<p>As a commercial and industrial city with a major port on the <a href="/wiki/Weser" title="Weser">River Weser</a>, Bremen is part of the <a href="/wiki/Bremen/Oldenburg_Metropolitan_Region" title="Bremen/Oldenburg Metropolitan Region">Bremen/Oldenburg Metropolitan Region</a>, with 2.4 million people. Bremen is the second most populous city in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Germany" title="Northern Germany">Northern Germany</a> and eleventh in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bremen is a major cultural and economic hub in the northern regions of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>. Bremen is home to dozens of historical galleries and museums, ranging from historical sculptures to major art museums, such as the <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cbersee-Museum_Bremen" title="Übersee-Museum Bremen">Übersee-Museum Bremen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Bremen has a reputation as a working-class city.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Bremen is home to a large number of multinational companies and manufacturing centers. Companies headquartered in Bremen include the <a href="/wiki/Hachez" title="Hachez">Hachez</a> chocolate company and <a href="/wiki/Vector_Foiltec" title="Vector Foiltec">Vector Foiltec</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Four-time <a href="/wiki/List_of_German_football_champions" title="List of German football champions">German football champions</a> <a href="/wiki/Werder_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Werder Bremen">Werder Bremen</a> are also based in the city.</p>
<p>Bremen is some 60&#160;km (37&#160;mi) south of the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Weser" title="Weser">Weser</a> on the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. Bremen and <a href="/wiki/Bremerhaven" title="Bremerhaven">Bremerhaven</a> (at the mouth of the Weser) together comprise the <a href="/wiki/States_of_Germany" title="States of Germany">state</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_(state)" title="Bremen (state)">Free Hanseatic City of Bremen</a> (official German name: <i>Freie Hansestadt Bremen</i>).</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Bremen" title="Timeline of Bremen">Timeline of Bremen</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" width="50" height="17" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="60" data-file-height="20" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that this section be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a> out into another article titled <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Bremen">History of Bremen</a></i>. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Bremen" title="Talk:Bremen">Discuss</a>) <small><i>(May 2017)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg/220px-Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg" width="220" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg/330px-Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg/440px-Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1048" data-file-height="507" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Braun_Bremen_UBHD.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bremen, 16th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The marshes and moraines near Bremen have been settled since about 12,000 BC. Burial places and settlements in Bremen-Mahndorf and Bremen-Osterholz date back to the 7th century AD. Since the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, some scientists have believed that the entry <i>Fabiranum</i> or <i>Phabiranon</i> in <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <i>Fourth Map of Europe</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> written in AD 150, refers to Bremen. But Ptolemy gives geographic coordinates, and these refer to a site northeast of the mouth of the river Visurgis (Weser). In Ptolemy's time the <a href="/wiki/Chauci" title="Chauci">Chauci</a> lived in the area now called north-western <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Lower Saxony</a>. By the end of the 3rd century, they had merged with the <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Wars" title="Saxon Wars">Saxon Wars</a> (772–804) the Saxons, led by <a href="/wiki/Widukind" title="Widukind">Widukind</a>, fought against the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West</a> <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a>, and lost the war.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_kings" title="List of Frankish kings">King of the Franks</a>, made a new law, the <a href="/wiki/Lex_Saxonum" title="Lex Saxonum">Lex Saxonum</a>, which forbid the Saxons worshipping <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a> (the god of the Saxons); instead they had to convert to Christianity on pain of death. In 787 <a href="/wiki/Willehad_of_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Willehad of Bremen">Willehad of Bremen</a> became the first Bishop of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bishopric_of_Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bishopric of Bremen (page does not exist)">Bremen</a>. In 848 the <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese">archdiocese</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> merged with the <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">diocese</a> of Bremen to become <a href="/wiki/Hamburg-Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamburg-Bremen">Hamburg-Bremen</a> Archdiocese, with its seat in Bremen, and in the following centuries the archbishops of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Archbishopric_of_Hamburg-Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Archbishopric of Hamburg-Bremen (page does not exist)">Hamburg-Bremen</a> were the driving force behind the <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianisation</a> of Northern Germany. In 888, at the behest of Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Rimbert" title="Rimbert">Rimbert</a>, Kaiser <a href="/wiki/Arnulf_of_Carinthia" title="Arnulf of Carinthia">Arnulf of Carinthia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingian</a> King of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_East_Francia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kingdom of East Francia (page does not exist)">East Francia</a>, granted Bremen the rights to hold its own markets, mint its own coins and make its own customs laws.</p>
<p>The city's first stone walls were built in 1032. Around that time trade with Norway, England and the northern Netherlands began to grow, thus increasing the importance of the city.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Germania_-_New_York,_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ancient_Germania_-_New_York%2C_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg/220px-Ancient_Germania_-_New_York%2C_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ancient_Germania_-_New_York%2C_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg/330px-Ancient_Germania_-_New_York%2C_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Ancient_Germania_-_New_York%2C_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg/440px-Ancient_Germania_-_New_York%2C_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1964" data-file-height="1619" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ancient_Germania_-_New_York,_Harper_and_Brothers_1849.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Germania, in the early 2nd century (Harper and Brothers, 1849).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG/220px-Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG/330px-Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG/440px-Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2880" data-file-height="2160" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bremen_aerial_view_9.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View from the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Cathedral" title="Bremen Cathedral">Bremen Cathedral</a> in the direction of the Stephani-Bridge.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1186 the Bremian <a href="/wiki/Prince-Bishop" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince-Bishop">Prince-Archbishop</a> <a href="/wiki/Hartwig_of_Uthlede" title="Hartwig of Uthlede">Hartwig of Uthlede</a> and his bailiff in Bremen confirmed - without generally waiving the prince-archbishop's overlordship over the city - the <a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gelnhauser_Privileg" class="extiw" title="de:Gelnhauser Privileg">Gelnhausen Privilege</a>, by which <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I <i>Barbarossa</i></a> granted the city considerable privileges. The city was recognised as a political entity with its own laws. Property within the municipal boundaries could not be subjected to feudal overlordship; this also applied to serfs who acquired property, if they lived in the city for a year and a day, after which they were to be regarded as free persons. Property was to be freely inherited without feudal claims for reversion to its original owner. This privilege laid the foundation for Bremen's later status of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">imperial immediacy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">Free Imperial City</a>).</p>
<p>But in reality Bremen did not have complete independence from the Prince-Archbishops: there was no <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">burghers</a> still had to pay taxes to the Prince-Archbishops. Bremen played a double role: it participated in the <a href="/wiki/Landtag#Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Landtag">Diets</a> of the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Bremen">Prince-Archbishopric of Bremen</a> as part of the Bremian <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_the_realm" title="Estates of the realm">Estates</a> and paid its share of taxes, at least when it had previously consented to this levy. Since the city was the major taxpayer, its consent was generally sought.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">for what?</span></a></i>]</sup> In this way the city wielded fiscal and <a href="/wiki/Political_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Political power">political power</a> within the Prince-Archbishopric, while not allowing the Prince-Archbishopric to rule in the city against its consent. In 1260 Bremen joined the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advent_of_territorial_power">Advent of territorial power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Advent of territorial power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png/240px-Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png" width="240" height="344" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png/360px-Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png/480px-Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png 2x" data-file-width="918" data-file-height="1316" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landgebiete_der_Freien_Stadt_Bremen.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
14th to 18th century: territories of the Free City of Bremen (red) and of the <a href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_Bremen" title="Archbishopric of Bremen">Archbishopric of Bremen</a> (yellow); straits between lower <a href="/wiki/Weser" title="Weser">Weser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jadebusen" class="mw-redirect" title="Jadebusen">Jadebusen</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1350, the number of inhabitants reached 20,000. Around this time the <i>Hansekogge</i> (<a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">cog ship</a>) became a unique product of Bremen.</p>
<p>In 1362, representatives of Bremen rendered homage to <a href="/wiki/Albert_of_Brunswick_and_Lunenburg-Wolfenb%C3%BCttel" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert of Brunswick and Lunenburg-Wolfenbüttel">Albert II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Bremen">Prince-Archbishop of Bremen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Langwedel" title="Langwedel">Langwedel</a>. In return, Albert confirmed the city's privileges and brokered a peace between the city and Gerhard III, Count <a href="/wiki/County_of_Hoya" title="County of Hoya">of Hoya</a>, who since 1358 had held some burghers of Bremen in captivity. The city had to bail them out. In 1365 an extra tax, levied to finance the ransom, caused an uprising among the burghers and artisans that was put down by the <a href="/wiki/City_council" title="City council">city council</a> after much bloodshed.</p>
<p>In 1366, Albert II tried to take advantage of the dispute between <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Bremen" title="Senate of Bremen">Bremen's city council</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a>, whose members had expelled some city councillors from the city. When these councillors appealed to Albert II for help, many artisans and burghers regarded this as a treasonous act, fearing that this appeal to the prince would only provoke him to abolish the autonomy of the city.</p>
<p>The fortified city maintained its own guards, not allowing soldiers of the Prince-Archbishop to enter it. The city reserved an extra very narrow gate, the so-called <i>Bishop's Needle</i> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Acus episcopi</i></span>, first mentioned in 1274), for all clergy, including the Prince-Archbishop. The narrowness of the gate made it physically impossible for him to enter surrounded by his knights.</p>
<p>Nevertheless, on the night of 29 May 1366, Albert's troops, helped by some burghers, invaded the city. Afterward, the city had to again render him homage: the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Roland" title="Bremen Roland">Bremen Roland</a>, symbol of the city's autonomy, was destroyed; and a new city council was appointed. In return, the new council granted Albert a credit amounting to the then-enormous sum of 20,000 Bremen marks.</p>
<p>But city councillors of the previous council, who had fled to the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Oldenburg" title="Duchy of Oldenburg">County of Oldenburg</a>, gained the support of the counts and recaptured the city on June 27, 1366. The members of the intermediate council were regarded as traitors and beheaded, and the city <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> regained its autonomy. Thereupon, the city of Bremen, which had for a long time held an autonomous status, acted almost completely independent of the Prince-Archbishop. Albert failed to obtain control over the city of Bremen a second time, since he was always short of money and lacked the support of his family, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Welf" title="House of Welf">Welfs</a>, who were preparing for and fighting the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg_War_of_Succession" class="mw-redirect" title="Lüneburg War of Succession">Lüneburg War of Succession</a> (1370–88).</p>
<p>By the end of the 1360s Bremen had provided credit to Albert II to finance his lavish lifestyle, and gained in return the <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6rde_Castle" class="mw-redirect" title="Vörde Castle">fortress of Vörde</a> along with the dues levied in its bailiwick as guarantee for the credit. In 1369 Bremen again lent money to Albert II against the collateral of his mint, which was from then on run by the city council, which took over his right to mint coins. In 1377 Bremen purchased from Duke <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Duke_of_Brunswick-L%C3%BCneburg" title="Frederick I, Duke of Brunswick-Lüneburg">Frederick I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brunswick-L%C3%BCneburg" title="Duchy of Brunswick-Lüneburg">Brunswick-Lüneburg</a> many of the Prince-Archbishop's castles, which Albert had pledged as security for a loan to Frederick's predecessor. Thus Bremen gained a powerful position in the Prince-Archbishopric (ecclesiastical principality), in effect sidelining its actual ruler.</p>
<p>The declining knightly family of <a href="/wiki/Bad_Bederkesa" title="Bad Bederkesa">Bederkesa</a> had become deeply indebted,<sup id="cite_ref-Edert_2010_29_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edert_2010_29-8">[8]</a></sup> and, having already sold many of their possessions, had even pawned half their say in the Bailiwick of Bederkesa (Amt Bederkesa) to the aspiring Mandelsloh family (a noble house, or <i>Adelsgeschlecht</i>). They lost the rest of their claims to the city of Bremen, when in 1381 its troops prevented the three Mandelsloh brothers from lending them to Albert II as territorial power.<sup id="cite_ref-Edert_2010_30_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edert_2010_30-9">[9]</a></sup> The Mandelslohs and other <a href="/wiki/Robber_baron_(feudalism)" title="Robber baron (feudalism)">robber barons</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Verden_(state)" title="Verden (state)">Prince-Bishopric of Verden</a> and the Prince-Archbishopric of Bremen ravaged burghers of the city of Bremen as well as inhabitants throughout the Prince-Archbishopric.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bederkesa,_Burg_3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Bederkesa%2C_Burg_3.JPG/220px-Bederkesa%2C_Burg_3.JPG" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Bederkesa%2C_Burg_3.JPG/330px-Bederkesa%2C_Burg_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Bederkesa%2C_Burg_3.JPG/440px-Bederkesa%2C_Burg_3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1007" data-file-height="575" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bederkesa,_Burg_3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bederkesa Castle, since 1381 a stronghold of Bremen's rural possessions within the Prince-Archbishopric, the later secularised Duchy of Bremen</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1381 the city's troops successfully ended the <a href="/wiki/Brigandage" title="Brigandage">brigandage</a> and captured the Castle of Bederkesa and its bailiwick. Thus Bremen gained a foothold to uphold peace and order in its forecourt on the lower course of the <a href="/wiki/Weser" title="Weser">Weser</a>. In 1386 the city of Bremen became the <a href="/wiki/Liege_lord" class="mw-redirect" title="Liege lord">liege lord</a> of the noble families holding the estates of Altluneburg and <a href="/wiki/Elmlohe" title="Elmlohe">Elmlohe</a>, who had previously been <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassals</a> of the Knights of Bederkesa. The city replaced in 1404 the old wooden statue of <a href="/wiki/Roland" title="Roland">Roland</a>, which had been destroyed in 1366 by the Bederkesa, with a larger limestone model; this statue has managed to survive six centuries and two World Wars into the 21st.</p>
<p>In 1411 the jointly ruling dukes of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxe-Lauenburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Duchy of Saxe-Lauenburg">Saxe-Lauenburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_IV,_Duke_of_Saxe-Lauenburg" title="Eric IV, Duke of Saxe-Lauenburg">Eric IV</a> and his sons <a href="/wiki/Eric_V,_Duke_of_Saxe-Lauenburg" title="Eric V, Duke of Saxe-Lauenburg">Eric V</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_IV,_Duke_of_Saxe-Lauenburg" title="John IV, Duke of Saxe-Lauenburg">John IV</a>, pawned their share in the Bederkesa bailiwick and castle to the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Bremen" title="Senate of Bremen">Senate of Bremen</a>, including all "they have in the jurisdictions in the Frisian <a href="/wiki/Land_Wursten" title="Land Wursten">Land of Wursten</a> and in Lehe (Bremerhaven), which belongs to the aforementioned castle and Vogtei".<sup id="cite_ref-Hucker_1972_13_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hucker_1972_13-10">[10]</a></sup> Their share in jurisdiction, <i>Vogtei</i> (bailiwick) and castle had been acquired from the plague-stricken Knights of Bederkesa.<sup id="cite_ref-Hucker_1972_13_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hucker_1972_13-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1421, Bremen acquired also the remaining half of the rights of the Bederkesa knights, including their remaining share in Bederkesa Castle.<sup id="cite_ref-Edert_2010_30_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edert_2010_30-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1648 the Prince-Archbishopric was transformed into the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Duchy of Bremen">Duchy of Bremen</a>, which was first ruled in <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a> by the Swedish Crown. In November 1654, after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bremian_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Bremian War">Second Bremian War</a>, Bremen had to cede Bederkesa and the settlement of Lehe to the Duchy of Bremen (Treaty of Stade, 1654).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bremen_and_the_Reformation">Bremen and the Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Bremen and the Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg/220px-Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg/330px-Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg/440px-Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1522" data-file-height="1141" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rathaus_Bremen_116thd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bremen town hall</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> swept through <a href="/wiki/Northern_Germany" title="Northern Germany">Northern Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Cathedral" title="Bremen Cathedral">St Peter's cathedral</a> belonged to the <i>cathedral <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_immunity" title="Sovereign immunity">immunity district</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Domfreiheit</span>; cf. also <a href="/wiki/Liberty_(division)" title="Liberty (division)">Liberty</a>), an extraterritorial enclave of the neighbouring Prince-Archbishopric of Bremen. In 1532, the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_chapter" title="Cathedral chapter">cathedral chapter</a> which was still Catholic at that time closed St Peter's after a mob consisting of Bremen's <a href="/wiki/Citizen" class="mw-redirect" title="Citizen">burghers</a> had forcefully interrupted a Catholic Mass and prompted a pastor to hold a <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> service.</p>
<p>In 1547, the chapter, which had in the meantime become predominantly Lutheran, appointed the Dutch <a href="/wiki/Albert_Hardenberg" title="Albert Hardenberg">Albert Rizaeus</a>, called Hardenberg, as the first Cathedral pastor of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> affiliation. Rizaeus turned out to be a partisan of the <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Theology of Huldrych Zwingli">Zwinglian</a> understanding of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Lord's Supper</a>, which was rejected by the then Lutheran majority of burghers, the city council, and chapter. So in 1561 - after heated disputes - Rizaeus was dismissed and banned from the city and the cathedral again closed its doors.</p>
<p>However, as a consequence of that controversy the majority of Bremen's burghers and city council adopted <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> by the 1590s, while the chapter, which was at the same time the body of secular government in the neighbouring Prince-Archbishopric, clung to <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a>. This antagonism between a Calvinistic majority and a Lutheran minority, though it had a powerful position in its immunity district (<a href="/wiki/German_Mediatisation" class="mw-redirect" title="German Mediatisation">mediatised</a> as part of the city in 1803), remained dominant until in 1873 the Calvinist and Lutheran congregations of Bremen were reconciled and founded a <a href="/wiki/United_and_uniting_churches" title="United and uniting churches">united</a> administrative umbrella <a href="/wiki/Bremische_Evangelische_Kirche" class="mw-redirect" title="Bremische Evangelische Kirche">Bremen Protestant Church</a>, which still exists today, comprising the bulk of Bremen's burghers.</p>
<p>At the beginning of the 17th century, Bremen continued to play its double role, wielding fiscal and political power within the Prince-Archbishopric, but not allowing the Prince-Archbishopric to rule in the city without its consent.</p>
<h3><span id="Thirty_Years'_War"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thirty_Years.27_War">Thirty Years' War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Thirty Years' War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soon after the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> Bremen declared its neutrality, as did most of the territories in the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxon_Circle" title="Lower Saxon Circle">Lower Saxon Circle</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Frederick_of_Holstein-Gottorp,_Prince-Bishop" title="John Frederick of Holstein-Gottorp, Prince-Bishop">John Frederick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Administrator_(prince-bishopric)" class="mw-redirect" title="Administrator (prince-bishopric)">Lutheran Administrator</a> of the Prince-Archbishopric of Bremen, desperately tried to keep his Prince-Archbishopric out of the war, with the complete agreement of the Estates and the city of Bremen. When in 1623 the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Republic of the Seven United Netherlands</a>, which was fighting in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Revolt" title="Dutch Revolt">Eighty Years' War</a> for its independence against <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburg</a>'s Spanish and imperial forces, requested its <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> co-religionist Bremen to join them, the city refused, but started to reinforce its fortifications.</p>
<p>In 1623 the territories comprising the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxon_Circle" title="Lower Saxon Circle">Lower Saxon Circle</a> decided to recruit an army in order to maintain an <a href="/wiki/Armed_neutrality" title="Armed neutrality">armed neutrality</a>, since troops of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_League_(German)" title="Catholic League (German)">Catholic League</a> were already operating in the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Lower_Rhenish-Westphalian_Circle" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower Rhenish-Westphalian Circle">Lower Rhenish-Westphalian Circle</a> and dangerously close to their region. The concomitant effects of the war, <a href="/wiki/Debasement" title="Debasement">debasement</a> of the currency and rising prices, had already caused inflation which was also felt in Bremen.</p>
<p>In 1623 the Republic of the Seven United Netherlands, diplomatically supported by King <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Brother-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Brother-in-law">brother-in-law</a> of <a href="/wiki/Christian_IV_of_Denmark" title="Christian IV of Denmark">Christian IV of Denmark</a>, started a new anti-Habsburg campaign. Thus the troops of the Catholic League were otherwise occupied and Bremen seemed relieved. But soon after this the imperial troops under <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_von_Wallenstein" title="Albrecht von Wallenstein">Albrecht von Wallenstein</a> headed north in an attempt to destroy the fading <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, in order to reduce the Hanseatic cities of Bremen, <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_L%C3%BCbeck" title="Free City of Lübeck">Lübeck</a> and to establish a Baltic trade monopoly, to be run by some imperial favourites including Spaniards and Poles. The idea was to win <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>'s and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>'s support, both of which had for a long time sought the destruction of the <i>Hanseatic League</i>.</p>
<p>In May 1625, Duke <a href="/wiki/Christian_IV_of_Denmark" title="Christian IV of Denmark">Christian IV</a> of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Holstein" title="Duchy of Holstein">Holstein</a> was elected - in the latter of his functions - by the Lower Saxon Circle's member territories <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-chief" title="Commander-in-chief">commander-in-chief</a> of the <a href="/wiki/West_Low_German" title="West Low German">Lower Saxon</a> troops. In the same year Christian IV joined the Anglo-Dutch military coalition. Christian IV ordered his troops to capture all the important traffic hubs in the Prince-Archbishopric and commenced the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lutter" title="Battle of Lutter">Battle of Lutter</a> am Barenberge, on 27 August 1626, where he was defeated by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_League_(German)" title="Catholic League (German)">Leaguist</a> troops under <a href="/wiki/Johann_Tserclaes,_Count_of_Tilly" title="Johann Tserclaes, Count of Tilly">Johan 't Serclaes, Count of Tilly</a>. Christian IV and his surviving troops fled to the Prince-Archbishopric and established their headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Stade" title="Stade">Stade</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bremen_Roland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Bremen_Roland.jpg/170px-Bremen_Roland.jpg" width="170" height="403" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Bremen_Roland.jpg/255px-Bremen_Roland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Bremen_Roland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="274" data-file-height="650" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bremen_Roland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Roland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1627 Christian IV withdrew from the Prince-Archbishopric, in order to oppose Wallenstein's invasion of his <a href="/wiki/Holstein" title="Holstein">Duchy of Holstein</a>. Tilly then invaded the Prince-Archbishopric and captured its southern part. Bremen shut its <a href="/wiki/City_gate" title="City gate">city gates</a> and entrenched itself behind its improved fortifications. In 1628, Tilly turned on the city, and Bremen paid him a ransom of 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Rixdollar" title="Rixdollar">rixdollars</a> in order to spare it a siege. The city remained unoccupied throughout the war.</p>
<p>The takeover by the Catholic League enabled <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand II, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand II, Holy Roman Emperor</a>, to implement the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Restitution" title="Edict of Restitution">Edict of Restitution</a>, decreed March 6, 1629, within the Prince-Archbishopric of Bremen including the city of Bremen. In September 1629 <a href="/wiki/Franz_Wilhelm_von_Wartenberg" title="Franz Wilhelm von Wartenberg">Francis William of Wartenberg</a>, appointed by Ferdinand II as chairman of the imperial restitution commission for the Lower Saxon Circle, in carrying out the provisions of the Edict of Restitution, ordered the Bremian Chapter, seated in Bremen, to render an account of all the capitular and prince-archiepiscopal <a href="/wiki/Estate_(law)" title="Estate (law)">estates</a> (not to be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Estates_of_the_realm" title="Estates of the realm">Estates</a>). The Chapter refused, arguing first that the order had not been authorised and later that due to disputes with Bremen's city council, they could not freely travel to render an account, let alone do the necessary research on the estates. The anti-Catholic attitudes of Bremen's burghers and council was to make it completely impossible to prepare the restitution of estates from the Lutheran Chapter to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>. Even Lutheran capitulars were uneasy in Calvinistic Bremen.</p>
<p>Bremen's city council ordered that the capitular and prince-archiepiscopal <a href="/wiki/Estate_(law)" title="Estate (law)">estates</a> within the boundaries of the unoccupied city were not to be restituted to the Catholic Church. The council argued that the city had long been Protestant, but the restitution commission replied that the city was <a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a> a part of the Prince-Archbishopric, so <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> had illegitimately taken over Catholic-owned estates. The city council replied that under these circumstances it would rather separate from the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and join the quasi-independent <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Republic of the Seven Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The city was neither to be conquered nor to be successfully besieged due to its new fortifications and its access to the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.</p>
<p>In October 1631 an army, newly recruited by John Frederick, started to reconquer the Prince-Archbishopric — helped by forces from Sweden and the city of Bremen. John Frederick returned to office, only to implement the supremacy of Sweden, insisting that it retain supreme command until the end of the war. With the impending enforcement of the military Major Power of Sweden over the Prince-Archbishopric of Bremen, which was under negotiation at the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Treaty of Westphalia</a>, the city of Bremen feared it would fall under Swedish rule too. Therefore, the city appealed for an imperial confirmation of its status of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">imperial immediacy</a> from 1186 (Gelnhausen Privilege). In 1646 <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand III, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand III, Holy Roman Emperor</a>, granted the requested confirmation (<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linzer_Diplom" class="extiw" title="de:Linzer Diplom">Diploma of Linz</a>) to the <a href="/wiki/Free_imperial_city" title="Free imperial city">Free Imperial City</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Swedish_reaction">Swedish reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Swedish reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<p>Nevertheless, Sweden, represented by its imperial fief <a href="/wiki/Bremen-Verden" title="Bremen-Verden">Bremen-Verden</a>, which comprised the secularised prince-bishoprics of Bremen and Verden, did not accept the imperial immediacy of the city of Bremen. Swedish Bremen-Verden tried to <a href="/wiki/Mediatization" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediatization">remediatise</a> the Free Imperial City of Bremen (i.e., to make it switch its allegiance to Sweden). With this in view, Swedish Bremen-Verden twice waged war on Bremen. In 1381 the city of Bremen had imposed de facto rule in an area around <a href="/wiki/Bad_Bederkesa" title="Bad Bederkesa">Bederkesa</a> and west of it as far as the lower branch of the <a href="/wiki/Weser" title="Weser">Weser</a> near Bremerlehe (a part of present-day Bremerhaven). Early in 1653, Bremen-Verden's Swedish troops captured Bremerlehe by force. In February 1654 the city of Bremen managed to get <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand III, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand III, Holy Roman Emperor</a>, to grant it a seat and the vote in the Holy Roman Empire's <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reichstag (Holy Roman Empire)">Diet</a>, thus accepting the city's status as Free Imperial City of Bremen.</p>
<p>Ferdinand III demanded that <a href="/wiki/Christina_of_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="Christina of Sweden">Christina of Sweden</a>, Duchess regnant of Bremen-Verden, compensate the city of Bremen for the damages caused and restitute Bremerlehe. When in March 1654 the city of Bremen started to recruit soldiers in the area of Bederkesa, in order to prepare for further arbitrary acts, Swedish Bremen-Verden enacted the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Wars_on_Bremen" title="Swedish Wars on Bremen">First Bremian War</a> (March to July 1654), arguing that it was acting in <a href="/wiki/Self-defense" title="Self-defense">self-defence</a>. The Free Imperial City of Bremen had meanwhile urged Ferdinand III to support it, who in July 1654 asked <a href="/wiki/Charles_X_Gustav_of_Sweden" title="Charles X Gustav of Sweden">Charles X Gustav of Sweden</a>, Christina's successor as Duke of Bremen-Verden, to cease the conflict, which resulted in the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recess_of_Stade_(1654)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Recess of Stade (1654) (page does not exist)">First Stade Recess</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erster_Stader_Vergleich" class="extiw" title="de:Erster Stader Vergleich">de</a>)</span> (November 1654). This treaty left the main issue, the acceptance of the city of Bremen's imperial immediacy, unresolved. But the city agreed to pay tribute and levy taxes in favour of Swedish Bremen-Verden and to cede its possessions around Bederkesa and Bremerlehe, which was why it was later called Lehe.</p>
<p>In December 1660 the city council of Bremen rendered homage as Free Imperial City of Bremen to <a href="/wiki/Leopold_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Leopold I, Holy Roman Emperor">Leopold I, Holy Roman Emperor</a>. In 1663 the city gained a seat and a vote in the Imperial Diet, despite sharp protest from Swedish Bremen-Verden. In March 1664 the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Sweden">Swedish Diet</a> came out in favour of waging war on the Free Imperial City of Bremen. Right after Leopold I, who was busy with wars against the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, had <a href="/wiki/Enfeoffing" class="mw-redirect" title="Enfeoffing">enfeoffed</a> the minor King <a href="/wiki/Charles_XI_of_Sweden" title="Charles XI of Sweden">Charles XI of Sweden</a> with Bremen-Verden, while the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg-Celle" class="mw-redirect" title="Lüneburg-Celle">Brunswick and Lunenburg-Celle</a> was occupied by succession quarrels and France not opposed, Sweden started the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Wars_on_Bremen" title="Swedish Wars on Bremen">Second Bremian War</a> (1665–66) from its Bremen-Verden fief.</p>
<p>The Swedes under <a href="/wiki/Carl_Gustaf_Wrangel" title="Carl Gustaf Wrangel">Carl Gustaf Wrangel</a> laid siege to the city of Bremen. The siege brought <a href="/wiki/Brandenburg-Prussia" title="Brandenburg-Prussia">Brandenburg-Prussia</a>, Brunswick and Lunenburg-Celle, Denmark, Leopold I and the Netherlands onto the scene, who were all in favour of the city, with Brandenburgian, Cellean, Danish, and Dutch troops at Bremen-Verden's borders ready to invade. So on 15 November 1666 Sweden had to sign the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Habenhausen" title="Treaty of Habenhausen">Treaty of Habenhausen</a>, obliging it to destroy the fortresses built close to Bremen and banning Bremen from sending its representative to the <i>Diet</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxon_Circle" title="Lower Saxon Circle">Lower Saxon Circle</a>. From then on no further Swedish attempts were made to capture the city.</p>
<p>In 1700 Bremen introduced - like all Protestant territories of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">imperial immediacy</a> - the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Improved Calendar</a>, as it was called by Protestants, in order not to mention the name of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">Pope Gregory XIII</a>. So Sunday, 18 February of Old Style was followed by Monday, 1 March <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">New Style</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png/240px-Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png" width="240" height="386" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png/360px-Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png/480px-Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png 2x" data-file-width="656" data-file-height="1056" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bremer_Staatsgebiet_seit_1800.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Territory of Bremen as state since 1800</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The harbour of Vegesack became part of the city of Bremen in 1803. In 1811, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> invaded Bremen and integrated it as the capital of the <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">Département de <a href="/wiki/Bouches_du_Weser" title="Bouches du Weser">Bouches-du-Weser</a></span> (Department of the Mouths of the Weser) into the French State. In 1813, the French - as they retreated - withdrew from Bremen. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Smidt" title="Johann Smidt">Johann Smidt</a>, Bremen's representative at the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a>, was successful in achieving the non-mediatisation of Bremen, <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_L%C3%BCbeck" title="Free City of Lübeck">Lübeck</a>, by which they were not incorporated into neighbouring monarchies, but became sovereign republics. Bremen joined the <a href="/wiki/North_German_Confederation" title="North German Confederation">North German Confederation</a> in 1867 and four years later became an autonomous component state of the new-founded <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and its successors.</p>
<p>The first German steamship was manufactured in 1817 in the shipyard of Johann Lange. In 1827, Bremen, under Johann Smidt, its mayor at that time, purchased land from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Kingdom of Hanover</a>, to establish the city of <a href="/wiki/Bremerhaven" title="Bremerhaven">Bremerhaven</a> (Port of Bremen) as an outpost of Bremen because the river <a href="/wiki/Weser" title="Weser">Weser</a> was silting up. The shipping company <a href="/wiki/Norddeutscher_Lloyd" title="Norddeutscher Lloyd">Norddeutscher Lloyd</a> (NDL) was founded in 1857. Lloyd was a byword for commercial shipping and is now a part of <a href="/wiki/Hapag-Lloyd" title="Hapag-Lloyd">Hapag-Lloyd</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Beck%27s_Brewery" title="Beck's Brewery">Beck's Brewery</a> was founded in 1837 and remains in operation today as part of <a href="/wiki/Anheuser-Busch_InBev" title="Anheuser-Busch InBev">Anheuser-Busch InBev</a>. In 1872, the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Cotton_Exchange" title="Bremen Cotton Exchange">Bremen Cotton Exchange</a> was founded.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Soviet_Republic" title="Bremen Soviet Republic">Soviet Republic of Bremen</a> existed from January to February 1919 in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, before it was overthrown by Gerstenberg <i>Freikorps</i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg/220px-Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg/330px-Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg/440px-Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg 2x" data-file-width="787" data-file-height="516" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rathaus_Bremen_15111918.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Proclamation of the <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolutionary Republic">Revolutionary Republic</a> of Bremen (<i>Bremer Räterepublik</i>) in front of the town hall, 15 November 1918.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henrich_Focke" title="Henrich Focke">Henrich Focke</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Georg_Wulf&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Georg Wulf (page does not exist)">Georg Wulf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Werner_Naumann" title="Werner Naumann">Werner Naumann</a> founded <a href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf" title="Focke-Wulf">Focke-Wulf</a> Flugzeugbau AG in Bremen in 1923; the aircraft construction company as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> forms part of <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> a manufacturer of civil and military aircraft. <a href="/wiki/Borgward" title="Borgward">Borgward</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automobile manufacturer</a>, was founded in 1929, and is today part of <a href="/wiki/Daimler_AG" title="Daimler AG">Daimler AG</a>.</p>
<p>The villages of Grohn, Schönebeck, Aumund, Hammersbeck, Fähr, Lobbendorf, Blumenthal, Farge and Rekum became part of the city of Bremen in 1939. The <a href="/wiki/Bremen-Vegesack" title="Bremen-Vegesack">Bremen-Vegesack</a> concentration camp operated during World War II.</p>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Bremen_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Bremen in World War II">bombing of Bremen in World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/3rd_Infantry_Division_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="3rd Infantry Division (United Kingdom)">British 3rd Infantry Division</a> under <a href="/wiki/Lashmer_Whistler" title="Lashmer Whistler">General Whistler</a> captured Bremen in late April 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1946 Bremen's mayor <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Kaisen" title="Wilhelm Kaisen">Wilhelm Kaisen</a> (SPD) travelled to the U.S. to re-establish Bremen's statehood, as Bremen had traditionally been a <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a>, in order to prevent its incorporation into the state of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Lower Saxony</a> in the British zone of occupation. In 1947 the city became an enclave, part of the <a href="/wiki/Allied-occupied_Germany" title="Allied-occupied Germany">American occupation zone</a> surrounded by the British zone.</p>
<p>In 1947, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Mende&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Martin Mende (page does not exist)">Martin Mende</a> founded <a href="/wiki/Nordmende" title="Nordmende">Nordmende</a>, a manufacturer of entertainment electronics. The company existed until 1987. <a href="/wiki/OHB-System" class="mw-redirect" title="OHB-System">OHB-System</a>, a manufacturer of medium-sized space-flight <a href="/wiki/Satellites" class="mw-redirect" title="Satellites">satellites</a>, was founded in 1958.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bremen" title="University of Bremen">University of Bremen</a>, founded in 1971, is one of 11 institutions classed as an "Elite university" in Germany, and teaches approximately 23,500 people from 126 countries.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg/220px-RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg/330px-RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg/440px-RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RathausDomBuergerschaft-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bremen's city hall, cathedral and <i>Bürgerschaft</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Weserhb2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Weserhb2.jpg/220px-Weserhb2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Weserhb2.jpg/330px-Weserhb2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Weserhb2.jpg/440px-Weserhb2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Weserhb2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View from the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stephanibr%C3%BCcke&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Stephanibrücke (page does not exist)">Stephanibrücke</a> towards the city centre and cathedral</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bremen lies on both sides of the <a href="/wiki/River_Weser" class="mw-redirect" title="River Weser">River Weser</a>, about 60 kilometres (37 miles) upstream of its estuary on the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> and its transition to the Outer Weser by Bremerhaven. Opposite Bremen's <i>Altstadt</i> is the point where the "Middle Weser" becomes the "Lower Weser" and, from the area of Bremen's port, the river has been made navigable to ocean-going vessels. The region on the left bank of the Lower Weser, through which the <a href="/wiki/Ochtum" title="Ochtum">Ochtum</a> flows, is the Weser Marshes, the landscape on its right bank is part of the <a href="/wiki/Elbe-Weser_Triangle" class="mw-redirect" title="Elbe-Weser Triangle">Elbe-Weser Triangle</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Lesum" title="Lesum">Lesum</a>, and its tributaries, the <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCmme" title="Wümme">Wümme</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamme_(river)" title="Hamme (river)">Hamme</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sch%C3%B6nebecker_Aue" title="Schönebecker Aue">Schönebecker Aue</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blumenthaler_Aue&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Blumenthaler Aue (page does not exist)">Blumenthaler Aue</a>, are the downstream tributaries of the Weser.</p>
<p>The city's municipal area is about 38 kilometres (24 miles) long and 16 kilometres (10 miles) wide. In terms of area, Bremen is the thirteenth largest city in Germany; and in terms of population the second largest city in northwest Germany after Hamburg and the tenth largest in the whole of Germany <i>(see: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cities in Germany">List of cities in Germany</a>)</i>.</p>
<p>Bremen lies about 50 kilometres (31 miles) east of the city of <a href="/wiki/Oldenburg_(Oldenburg)" class="mw-redirect" title="Oldenburg (Oldenburg)">Oldenburg</a>, 110 kilometres (68 miles) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, 120 kilometres (75 miles) northwest of <a href="/wiki/Hanover" title="Hanover">Hanover</a>, 100 kilometres (62 miles) north of <a href="/wiki/Minden" title="Minden">Minden</a> and 105 kilometres (65 miles) northeast of <a href="/wiki/Osnabr%C3%BCck" title="Osnabrück">Osnabrück</a>. Part of <a href="/wiki/Bremerhaven" title="Bremerhaven">Bremerhaven</a>'s port territory forms an <a href="/wiki/Exclave" class="mw-redirect" title="Exclave">exclave</a> of the City of Bremen.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hills_of_Bremen">Hills of Bremen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hills of Bremen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The inner city lies on a Weser dune, which reaches a natural height of <span style="border-width:0px; padding:0px; margin:0px;">10.5&#160;m&#160;above&#160;<a href="/wiki/Normalnull" title="Normalnull">sea level (NN)</a></span> at <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Cathedral" title="Bremen Cathedral">Bremen Cathedral</a>; its highest point, though, is <span style="border-width:0px; padding:0px; margin:0px;">5&#160;m&#160;above&#160;<a href="/wiki/Normalnull" title="Normalnull">sea level (NN)</a></span> and lies to the east at the <i><a href="/wiki/Polizeihaus" class="mw-redirect" title="Polizeihaus">Polizeihaus</a></i>, <i>Am Wall</i> 196. The highest natural feature in the city of Bremen is <span style="border-width:0px; padding:0px; margin:0px;">32.5&#160;m&#160;above&#160;NN</span> and lies in <a href="/wiki/Friedehorst_Park" title="Friedehorst Park">Friedehorst Park</a> in the northwestern borough of Burglesum.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> As a result, Bremen has the lowest <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_highest_points_of_the_German_states" title="List of the highest points of the German states">high point of all the German states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> However, the man-made <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">tip</a> of the rubbish dump Blockland-Deponie in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Walle,_Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Walle, Bremen (page does not exist)">Bremen-Walle</a> is higher at <span style="border-width:0px; padding:0px; margin:0px;">49&#160;m&#160;above&#160;NN</span>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bremen has a moderate <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>Cfb</i>) due to its proximity to the North Sea coast and temperate maritime air masses that move in with the predominantly westerly winds from the Atlantic Ocean. However, periods in which continental air masses predominate may occur at any time of the year and can lead to heat waves in the summer and prolonged periods of frost in the winter. In general though, extremes are rare in Bremen and temperatures below −15&#160;°C (5.0&#160;°F) and above 35&#160;°C (95.0&#160;°F) occur only once every couple of years. The record high temperature was 37.6&#160;°C (99.7&#160;°F) on 9 August 1992, while the record low temperature was −23.6&#160;°C (−10.5&#160;°F) on 13 February 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Being at some distance from the main North Sea, Bremen still has a somewhat wider temperature range than <a href="/wiki/Bremerhaven" title="Bremerhaven">Bremerhaven</a> that is located on the mouth of Weser.</p>
<p>Average temperatures have risen continually over the last decades, leading to a 0.6&#160;°C (1.1&#160;°F) rise in the mean annual temperature between 1961–90 and 1981–2010 reference periods. As in most parts of Germany, the year 2014 has been the warmest year on record averaging 11.1&#160;°C (52.0&#160;°F), making Bremen the second-warmest German state after Berlin in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> While Bremen is located in the comparatively cloudy northwestern part of Germany, there has been a significant increase in average sunshine hours over the last decades, especially in the months of April, May and July, causing the annual mean to rise by 62 hours between the two reference periods mentioned above.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> This trend has continued over the last 10 years, which average 1614 hours of sunshine, a good 130 hours more than in the international reference period of 1961–90.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Nevertheless, especially the winters remain extremely gloomy by international standards with December averaging hardly more than one hour of sunshine (out of 7 astronomically possible) per day, a feature that Bremen shares with most of Germany and its neighbouring countries, though.</p>
<p>Precipitation is distributed fairly even around the year with a small peak in summer mainly due to convective precipitation, i.e. showers and thunderstorms. Snowfall and the period of snow cover are variable; whereas in some years, hardly any snow accumulation occurs, there has recently been a series of unusually snowy winters, peaking in the record year 2010 counting 84 days with a snow cover.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Nevertheless, snow accumulation of more than 20 centimetres (8&#160;in) remains exceptional, the record being 68 centimetres (26.8&#160;in) of snow on 18 February 1979.</p>
<p>The warmest months in Bremen are June, July, and August, with average high temperatures of 20.2 to 22.6&#160;°C (68.4 to 72.7&#160;°F). The coldest are December, January, and February, with average low temperatures of −1.1 to 0.3&#160;°C (30.0 to 32.5&#160;°F). Typical of its maritime location, autumn tends to remain mild well into October, while spring arrives later than in the southwestern parts of the country.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>Stadtbürgerschaft</i> (municipal assembly) is made up of 68 of the 83 legislators of the state legislature, the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bremische-buergerschaft.de/"><i>Bremische Bürgerschaft</i></a>, who reside in the city of Bremen. The legislature is elected by the citizens of Bremen every four years.</p>
<p>Bremen has a reputation as a <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">Left-wing</a> city. This left wing atmosphere largely stems from a transition from an industrial economy to a service economy.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In elections for the Stadtbürgerschaft, the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party</a> has dominated for decades. <a href="/wiki/Alliance_%2790/The_Greens" class="mw-redirect" title="Alliance '90/The Greens">The Greens</a> have also been very successful in city elections. The state of <a href="/wiki/Bremen_(state)" title="Bremen (state)">Bremen</a>, which consists of the city, is governed by a coalition of the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alliance_%2790/The_Greens" class="mw-redirect" title="Alliance '90/The Greens">The Greens</a>.</p>
<p>One of the two mayors (<i>Bürgermeister</i>) is elected <a href="/wiki/List_of_mayors_of_Bremen" title="List of mayors of Bremen">President of the Senate</a> (<i>Präsident des Senats</i>) and serves as head of the city and the state. The current President is <a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carsten_Sieling" class="extiw" title="de:Carsten Sieling">Carsten Sieling</a></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_sights">Main sights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bremen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Main sights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Many of the sights in Bremen are found in the <i>Altstadt</i> (Old Town), an oval area surrounded by the Weser River, on the southwest, and the <i>Wallgraben</i>, the former moats of the medieval city walls, on the northeast. The oldest part of the Altstadt is the southeast half, starting with the <i>Marktplatz</i> and ending at the <i><a href="/wiki/Schnoor" title="Schnoor">Schnoor</a> quarter</i>.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Bremer_Marktplatz" title="Bremer Marktplatz">Marktplatz</a></i> (Market square) is dominated by the opulent façade of the <a href="/wiki/Town_Hall_of_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Town Hall of Bremen">Town Hall of Bremen</a>. The building was erected between 1405 and 1410 in <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic style</a>, but the façade was built two centuries later (1609–12) in <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance style</a>. The Town Hall is the seat of the President of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Bremen" title="Senate of Bremen">Senate of Bremen</a>. Today, it hosts a restaurant in original decor with gigantic <a href="/wiki/Oak_(wine)" title="Oak (wine)">wine barrels</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Ratskeller" title="Bremen Ratskeller">Ratskeller in Bremen</a>, and the wine lists boasts more than 600 - exclusively German - wines. It is also home of the twelve oldest wines in the world, stored in their original barrels in the Apostel chamber. In July 2004, along with the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Roland" title="Bremen Roland">Bremen Roland</a>, the building was added to the list of <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Sites</a>.</li>
<li>Two statues stand to the west side of the Town Hall: one is the statue <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Roland" title="Bremen Roland">Bremen Roland</a> (1404) of the city's protector, <a href="/wiki/Roland" title="Roland">Roland</a>, with his view against the <i>Cathedral</i> and bearing Durendart, the "sword of justice" and a shield decorated with an <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">imperial eagle</a>. The other near the entrance to the <i>Ratskeller</i> is <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Marcks" title="Gerhard Marcks">Gerhard Marcks</a>' <a href="/wiki/Bronze_sculpture" title="Bronze sculpture">bronze sculpture</a> (1953) <i>Die Stadtmusikanten</i> (<a href="/wiki/Town_Musicians_of_Bremen" title="Town Musicians of Bremen">Town Musicians</a>), which portrays the donkey, dog, cat and rooster of the <a href="/wiki/Brothers_Grimm" title="Brothers Grimm">Grimm Brothers'</a> fairy tale.</li>
<li>Other interesting buildings in the vicinity of the Marktplatz are the <i><a href="/wiki/Sch%C3%BCtting_(Bremen)" title="Schütting (Bremen)">Schütting</a></i>, a 16th-century Flemish-inspired guild hall, <a href="/wiki/Rathscaf%C3%A9_(Bremen)" title="Rathscafé (Bremen)">Rathscafé</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raths-Apotheke_(Bremen)" title="Raths-Apotheke (Bremen)">Raths-Apotheke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haus_der_Stadtsparkasse_(Bremen)" title="Haus der Stadtsparkasse (Bremen)">Haus der Stadtsparkasse</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Stadtwaage_(Bremen)" title="Stadtwaage (Bremen)">Stadtwaage</a></i>, the former <a href="/wiki/Weigh_house" title="Weigh house">weigh house</a> (built in 1588), with an ornate Renaissance façade, and the nearby <a href="/wiki/Essighaus" title="Essighaus">Essighaus</a>, once a fine Renaissance town house. The façades and houses surrounding the market square were the first buildings in Bremen to be restored after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, by the citizens of Bremen themselves.</li>
<li>St Peter's <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Cathedral" title="Bremen Cathedral">Cathedral</a> (13th century), to the east of the Marktplatz, with sculptures of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> and <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a> and Charlemagne.</li>
<li>On Katherinenklosterhof to the northwest of the cathedral, a few remaining traces can be found of <a href="/wiki/St_Catherine%27s_Monastery,_Bremen" title="St Catherine's Monastery, Bremen">St Catherine's Monastery</a> dating back to the 13th century.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Church_of_Our_Lady_(Bremen)" title="Church of Our Lady (Bremen)">Liebfrauenkirche</a></i> (Our Lady's Church) is the oldest church of the town (11th century). Its crypt features several impressive murals from the 14th century.</li>
<li>Off the south side of the Markplatz, the 110&#160;m (120&#160;yd) <i><a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6ttcherstra%C3%9Fe" title="Böttcherstraße">Böttcherstraße</a></i> was transformed in 1923–1931 by the coffee magnate <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Roselius" title="Ludwig Roselius">Ludwig Roselius</a>, who commissioned local artists to convert the narrow street (in medieval times, the street of the barrel makers) into an inspired mixture of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a>. It was considered "<i>entartete Kunst</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Degenerate_art" title="Degenerate art">degenerate art</a>) by the Nazis. Today, the street is one of Bremen's most popular attractions, with the <a href="/wiki/Glockenspiel_House" title="Glockenspiel House">Glockenspiel House</a> at No. 4 with its <a href="/wiki/Carillon" title="Carillon">carillon</a> of <a href="/wiki/Meissen_porcelain" title="Meissen porcelain">Meissen porcelain</a> bells.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li>
<li>At the end of Böttcherstraße, by the Weser bank, stands the <i>Martinikirche</i> (St Martin's Church), a Gothic brick church built in 1229, and rebuilt in 1960 after its destruction in World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
<li>Tucked away between the Cathedral and the river is the <i><a href="/wiki/Schnoor" title="Schnoor">Schnoor</a></i>, a small, well-preserved area of crooked lanes, fishermen's and shipper's houses from the 17th and 18th centuries, now occupied by cafés, artisan shops and <a href="/wiki/Art_museum" title="Art museum">art galleries</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Convent_of_Saint_Birgitta,_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Convent of Saint Birgitta, Bremen">Convent of Saint Birgitta</a> (<i>Birgittenkloster</i>) founded in 2002 is a small community of just seven nuns offering guest accommodation.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Schlachte" class="mw-redirect" title="Schlachte">Schlachte</a>, the medieval <a href="/wiki/Harbor" title="Harbor">harbour</a> of Bremen (the modern port is some kilometres downstream) and today a riverside boulevard with pubs and bars aligned on one side and the banks of Weser on the other.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Viertel_(Bremen)" title="Viertel (Bremen)">Viertel</a> district to the east of the old town combines rows of 19th-century Bremen Houses (<i>Bremer Häuser</i>) with museums and theatres along the city's cultural mile.<sup id="cite_ref-dv_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dv-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i>Nasir Moschee</i> is the first purpose built mosque of the <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> Muslim Community in Bremen.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>More contemporary <a href="/wiki/Tourist_attraction" title="Tourist attraction">tourist attractions</a> include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Universum_Science_Center" title="Universum Science Center">Universum Science Center</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">modern science</a> museum</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Rhododendron-Park_Bremen" title="Rhododendron-Park Bremen">Rhododendron-Park Bremen</a>, a major collection of <a href="/wiki/Rhododendron" title="Rhododendron">rhododendrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azalea" title="Azalea">azaleas</a>, which also includes a <a href="/wiki/Botanical_garden" title="Botanical garden">botanical garden</a></li>
<li><i>Botanika</i>, a nature museum within the Rhododendron-Park Bremen that attempts be to the same as the <i>Universum</i>, but for biology</li>
<li><i>Beck's Brewery</i>, tours are available to the public which include <a href="/wiki/Beer_tasting" class="mw-redirect" title="Beer tasting">beer tasting</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Kunsthalle_Bremen" title="Kunsthalle Bremen">Kunsthalle Bremen</a>, an art museum with paintings from the 19th and 20th century, maintained by the citizens of Bremen</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.focke-museum.de/">Focke Museum</a>, People of Bremen's Museum for Art and Cultural History</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cbersee_Museum_Bremen" class="mw-redirect" title="Übersee Museum Bremen">Übersee Museum Bremen</a> (<i>Overseas (World) Museum</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Natural_History" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural History">Natural History</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnographic">Ethnographic</a> museum near by the <a href="/wiki/Bremen_Hauptbahnhof" title="Bremen Hauptbahnhof">Central Station Bremen</a></li>
<li>The <i>Kunstsammlungen Böttcherstraße</i>, an art museum in <a href="/wiki/Expressionist_architecture" title="Expressionist architecture">expressionist architecture</a> from <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Hoetger" title="Bernhard Hoetger">Bernhard Hoetger</a> with paintings from the 20th century from <a href="/wiki/Paula_Modersohn-Becker" title="Paula Modersohn-Becker">Paula Modersohn-Becker</a>.</li>
<li>The Weserburg Museum für moderne Kunst ("Weserburg Modern Art Museum"), a modern art museum located in the middle of the Weser River.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
